PREFACE 
Purits who have mastered the First Year’s work of the 
present French Course have learned — 
The elementary facts of grammar ; 
The conjugation of avoir, étre, and the four regular conjuga- 
tions ; 
Most of the connecting words of constant occurrence ; 
The translation of about seventy pagesof French into English ; 
A limited amount of translation of English into French ; 
and they have been trained — 
To pronounce fluently ; 
To think directly in French; 
To remember French sentences; and 
To understand spoken French. 
In this Second Year the work will be continued on these 
foundations. : 
Pupils who have mastered the present volume will be pre- 
pared to pass an examination in “Elementary French” for 
admission to universities, colleges, or scientific schools, as set 
forth in the preface to the first volume of this course.

FRENCH COURSE—SECOND YEAR 
LESSON I 
1. Pronunciation. — Utter distinctly — 
a, 6, i, 6, u, 
and pronounce them in the words — s 
Ame, été, fini, réti, élu. 
If necessary, review First Year, Lesson I. 
2. Class Practice for Fluency in Reading. — The follow- 
ing paragraph is intended for practice in reading fluently 
in class. It is not for preparation at home. The transla- 
tion is given to enable you to read the French intelligently. 
Tl passe quelquefois sur les campagnes un vent qui 
There passes sometimes over the fields a wind which 
desséche les plantes, et alors on voit leurs tiges 
dries up the plants, and then we see their stalks 
flétries pencher vers la terre; mais, humectées par la 
withered bend towards the ground; but, moistened by the 
rosée, elles reprennent leur fraicheur, relévent leur 
dew, they regain their Sreshness, lift again their 
téte languissante. Il y a toujours des vents brilants, 
drooping heads. There are always burning winds, 
qui passent sur lame de Vhomme, et la desséchent. 
which pass over man’s soul, and parch it. 
La priére est Ja rosée qui la rafraichit. 
Prayer is the dew which refreshes it.

2 FRENCH COURSE— SECOND YEAR 
3. Verbs. — Review the verb donner (see pages 4 and 5) 
by studying it, and writing it from memory. Bring the 
verb écouter, fo listen, written neatly in ink. With each 
tense, give the meaning of the first person singular in 
English. 
4. Verbs for Class Work.— Write on the blackboard (or on 
paper) the simple tenses of — 
lever _—to raise peser to weigh semer to sow 
acheter to buy achever to complete amener to bring 
In these verbs, observe the following peculiarity. The first person 
singular of the present ‘tense, for example, would be formed like je 
donne : 
je leve, je pese, je seme, j’achete, j’acheve, j’amene; 
but these words would be unpronounceable, because unaccented ¢ is 
silent at the end of a syllable, and they each have two such syllables 
in succession. The same would be true if they ended in es or ent. 
Hence the — 
Rule. — When the syllable preceding e, es, or ent ends in e, mark 
it with a grave accent (2). 
You must therefore write: je léve, je pése, j’achéte, etc.; tu léves, 
il léve, ils lévent; but you must not use the accent in vous levez, nous 
levons (pr. voul-vé, noul-von). 
Again, in all the persons of the future and the conditional, two 
consecutive syllables ending in silent e would occur; as, je leverai, je 
leverais, j’acheverai, j'acheverais. According to the same principle, 
write: je léverai, je léverais, j’'achéverai, j’achéverais. 
Pronounce the verbs you have written, paying special attention to 
the e’s. 
5. The Present Indicative.—-The present tense is used 
like the English present, except when the latter has a 
future meaning after when, while, as soon as, as long as, as, 
wherever, after. Thus, when we say, I will buy it for you 
when I go out, the French say when I shall go out. Quand 
je sortirai, je l’achéterai pour vous.

Translate orally into French: 1. We'll listen while you 
speak. 2. As soon as you raise your hands, I shall 
know (je saurai) that you are ready (préts). 3. As long 
as they are quiet (tranquilles), I shall be satisfied (content). 
4. When he brings (apporter) us the books, we shall pay 
(ayer) him. 5. Let us know (faites-nous savoir) as soon 
as they arrive (arriver). 6. I'll seal (cacheter) the letters 
while you put on (y mettre) the stamps (timbre). 7. We'll 
follow (suivre) you wherever you please. 8. Wait here 
while I speak to that gentleman. 9. When you hear 
three cannon shots (coups de canon), you will know (saurez) 
that the ship (le navire) has arrived (est arrivé). 10. I 
shall not forget your kindness (bonté) as long as I live 
(vivre). 11. As soon as you receive the money (Vargent), 
run to the bank (courez 4 la banque). 12. When the letter 
is finished, bring (apporter) it to me. 13. Who will take 
care of (aura soin de) the house while you are in Europe? 
14. As long as Jean serves (servir) us well, we shall keep 
(garder) him.

ing it, and writing it from memory. Bring the simple 
tenses of the verb réfléchir, to reflect, written out carefully 
as directed in Lesson I. 
7. Pronunciation. — Distinguish carefully ew and u, é 
and é, é and a, 6 and o. If necessary, review Lesson I 
and III, First Year, and then pronounce — 
peur, pure, pré, prés, éléve, apre, aprés, céte, cote. 
8. Class Practice for Fluency in Reading. — 
Avec de la vertu, de la capacité, et une bonne conduite, 
Even with virtue, capacity, and good behavior,

9. Verbs for Class Work. — Write on the blackboard or on paper 
the simple’ tenses of — 
appeler {0 call jeter to throw renouveler to renew 
In these and a few other verbs ending in eler and eter, a different 
remedy is applied to prevent the occurrence of two consecutive sylla- 
bles ending in silent ¢ (je jete, je renouvele); namely, the ¢ or the 
dis. doubled. Write: — 
je jette, tu jettes, il jette, ils jettent; je jetterai; je jetterais; 
j'appelle, etc.
-P-A-G-E-14
LESSON III T 
This device produces the same sound as the grave accent, since 
unaccented e followed by a consonant in the same syllable has the 
sound of é. 
Do not double / or ¢ when it is not necessary, but write : nous jetons, 
vous jetez, nous appelons, etc. 
10. Write out also (in class) the simple tenses of — 
céder to yield célébrer to celebrate espérer to hope 
régler to regulate répéter to repeat protéger to protect 
In these and all other infinitives whose penult (next to last syl- 
lable) contains ¢, change ¢ to @ before the silent endings e, es, ent. 
Write je céde, tu cédes, il cede; but nous cédons, etc. 
No French word contains the sound of ¢ before a final consonant 
sound, 
Do not change ¢ in the future or in the conditional, but write je 
céderai, je céderais. 
Pronounce the verbs you have written, paying special attention to 
e, é and 2

the simple tenses of the verb entendre, to hear, in ink, and

an, on, in, un.

8 FRENCH COURSE — SECOND YEAR 
13. Verbs for Class Work.— Write on the blackboard, or on 
paper ; — . 
employer to employ appuyer to press nettoyer to clean 
noyer to drown payer to pay balayer io sweep 
essuyer to wipe essayer to iry 
In verbs ending in oyer, uyer, the y is changed to i hefore silent 
e, es, ent. Examples: j’appuie, tu appuies, ils appuient, j’emploierai, 
je noierais; but nous employons, vous employez. 
In all verbs ending in yer, we obtain such forms as nous essuyions, 
vous payiez, in which y is followed by #. 
In verbs ending in ayer, some writers change y to i before silent e, 
es, ent, and some retain the y. 
14. The Conditional Mood. — The verb form je donnerais, 
vous donneriez, has been recited with the translation I should 
give... if, you would give... if. Its name, the condi- 
tional mood, is given to it, because it is generally accom- 
panied by a condition beginning with #f. : 
Je vous préterais mon eanif, I should lend you my knife 
si je pouvais le trouver af I could find it 
Observe that the condition after si is expressed in the 
imperfect indicative. 
Je préterais has in it the idea of present or future time. 
It is called the present or simple conditional (also first 
conditional). ~ 
15. The compound conditional is made up of the simple 
conditional of the auxiliary and a perfect participle : — 
Je vous aurais prété mon T should have lent you my | 
canif si javais pu le knife if Teould have found 
trouver it (had been able to find 
tt) 
This verb form refers to past time and is also called the 
past conditional, perfect conditional or second conditional.
-P-A-G-E-16
LESSON IIL 9 
Note. —The conditional of the auxiliary j'aurais, je serais, is some- 
times replaced by the imperfect subjunctive j’eusse and je fusse. These 
forms are then also used in the other part of the sentence, after si. 
Examples will be found in the reading lessons. 
16. The condition (if...) is sometimes expressed in 
other ways. Thus, I should not have succeeded without 
your help =if you had not helped me. 
Sometimes the condition is not mentioned, as in ques- 
tions: Would you help us? It is easy to see that some con- 
dition, such as if you could, if you had time, is understood. 
17. Oral Translation into French and Dictation. — As 
some participles require étre instead of avoir (Year J, Arts. 
470, 471), you must use je serais, etc., instead of jaurais, 
etc., for I should have, etc., in their compound conditional. 
Such verbs are here marked with an asterisk. 
1. He would be happier (plus heureux) if he had some- 
thing to do (4 faire). 2. If 1 were you (de vous), I 
should not give him anything (rien). 3. If they asked 
us, we should not answer. 4. They would have more 
patience (plus de p.), if they were here. 5. If you took 
(preniez) the six o’clock train (le train de six heures), you 
would arrive at nine (a neuf heures). 6. He would have 
been happier, if he had had something to do. 7. If I 
had been in your place, I should not have given him any- 
thing. 8. If you had taken (pris) the five o’clock train 
you would have arrived * at eight. 9. If the king had 
not yielded (cédé), there would have been (il y...) a 
revolution. 10. We should have gone out* if it had 
not rained (plu). 11. Would you have gone* with us 
if we had invited (invité) you? 12. He would have - 
come,* if he had been able (pu).

18. Written Hxercise.— Review the verb recevoir, by 
studying it, and writing it from memory. Article 19 
explains why some persons have a cedilla, and others 
have none (Cregois, recevez). Bring the simple tenses of 
apercevoir, to perceive. 
19. Pronunciation. — Remember that ¢ sounds like k 
before a, 0, @, and w; as in car, corde, coeur, curé. Before e, 
é, é, é, t, on the other hand, it sounds like g in so. Wxam- 
ples : ce, céder, sincére, ancétre, ici. 
Before a, 0, u the ¢ sound is spelled with ¢. 
Before e, é, 6, é, i the & sound is spelled with gu. 
Pronounce — 
ca = qué qui co cu 
: ga cé ci go gu 
20. The letter g sounds as in the English word go 
before a, 0, u, and like French j in je, jour, before the other 
vowels. Examples: gargon, gorge, aigu; gelé, géle, protégé, 
géne, gilet. 
Before e, é, é, é,7 the first sound (g in English go) is 
written gu. 
Before a and o the second sound (French 7) is written ge. 
Pronounce — 
ga gué gui go gu 
: gea = gé gi geo ju

22. Verbs for Class Work. — Write the simple tenses of —

Observe that in these verbs c has the sound of s, and g the sound 
of 7. These sounds must be preserved in all the persons. Therefore 
you must use ¢ and ge when necessary, and write, for example, nous 
forgons, nous mangeons instead of forcons and mangons, which would 
not sound as though they belonged to the same verbs.

LESSON V 
24, The Irregular Verbs. —A verb is called irregular 
when any of its parts cannot be formed by adding to its 
stem (also called “root”) the endings which belong to 
its conjugation. Prendre, fo take, for example, is irregular 
because we take is nous prenons instead of nous prendons.
-P-A-G-E-19
12 FRENCH COURSE— SECOND YEAR 
25. The author has discovered that the great majority 
of irregular verbs may be divided into two groups, | | 
because some of their tenses are derived in two differ- 
ent ways. 
We shall represent these two groups by the verbs : — 
Inf. Pres, Ind. Pres. Part. Past Part. 
I. Lire je lis lisant lu 
to read Iam reading reading read 
II. Kerire yécris écrivant écrit 
to write LIamvriting riting written    
  26. To Group I belong all verbs (with six exceptions) , 
whose past participles end in u or é. 
To Group II belong all verbs (with three exceptions) 
whose past participles end in ¢. 
This leaves but very few verbs that are too irregular to 
be classified. 
27. The four parts! given above must be learned for 
each verb, The other parts can easily be derived from 
them, as the following diagrams (from Kroeh’s French 
Verb) will show : —

28. Explanation of the Diagrams. 
1. From the infinitive, form the future and the condi- 
tional by adding at, ats, to the whole word, dropping final 
e when there is one : — 
lire = + ai = je lirai I shall read 
écrire + ai = j’écrirai L shall write 
dormir + ai = jedormirai J shail sleep 
So also je lirais, j’écrirais, je dormirais, I should read, write, 
sleep. 
2. From the first person singular of the present tense, 
je lis, j’écris, form the rest of the singular, just as je finis is 
followed by tu finis, il finit. 
The plural of the present tense, however, is formed by 
adding the regular endings (ons, ez, ent) to the stem of 
the present participle, which is found by cutting off ant. 
8. From the same stem lis, éeriv, are formed the pres- 
ent subjunctive by adding e: — 
que je lise that I may read 
que j’écrive that I may write 
and the imperfect indicative by adding ais : — 
je lisais J was reading fécrivais Iwas writing
-P-A-G-E-21
i4 FRENCH COURSE — SECOND YEAR 
4. From the past participle in « (or i) are formed the 
past definite by adding s : — 
lu+s=jelus Iread dormi +s = je dormis T slept 
and the imperfect subjunctive by adding sse : — 
lu + sse = que je lusse that I might read 
dormi + sse = que je dormisse that I might sleep 
From a past participle that ends in ¢ no tenses are 
formed. This is what distinguishes the two groups. 
In the second group form the past definite and the 
imperfect subjunctive from the stem of the present parti- 
ciple (éceriv), by adding is and isse : ~ 
écriv +is = j’écrivis I wrote 
écriv + isse = que j’écrivisse that I might write 
5. The other persons. — Having thus obtained the first 
person of each tense, the other persons follow as in regular 
verbs. 
je tu il nous yous ils

6. The imperative is taken from the present indicative, 
without change of endings : — 
lis read (thou) écris write (thou)

32. Translation.—- Here begins the study of Jules 
Verne’s interesting and instructive story, entitled Le 
Tour du Monde en Quatre-vingts Jours, “ Around the 
World in Eighty Days.” 
DIRECTIONS FOR STUDY 
1. Pronounce each French word mentally (or, better, 
aloud) and think of its meaning. Never look at a French 
word without pronouncing it. 
2. When you come to a new word, look in the foot- 
notes. 
8. When you come to the end of the phrase or of the 
sentence, stop long enough to be sure you understand it, 
before you go on. If it does not “ make sense,” the fault 
is yours and not Jules Verne’s. 
4. Learn the French words in the foot-notes, so that 
you will know their meaning when they occur again in 
another place. 
5. Then read the French over once (or several times) 
more. You must try to acquire the habit of understand- 
ing French when you read it rapidly in French. Do not 
regard the work advised here as “too much trouble.” It 
will save you much time in the end. 
6. As so much attention was paid during the First 
Year to the study of the “most important words,” they 
will now be omitted from the foot-notes; but an alpha- 
betical list of these First Year words, with their most
-P-A-G-E-24
LESSON VI 17 
usual translation, is given on p. 831. If you do not know 
them, you should learn them as soon as possible. Enter 
little phrases containing them in your note-book. Draw 
a line down the middle of the page. Write the transla- 
tion opposite. : 
If your memory for words is not good, add to your 
note-book the other words from the foot-notes and rehearse 
them frequently by covering the English with a card and 
recalling it at the sight of the French. 
The most important new words are in type which in- 
clines to the right. The way to become familiar with 
words is by seeing them frequently.

Fogg, esq., ’un des membres les plus singuliers et les

tout.®

informés ne pouvaient dire, et Mr. Fogg était le dernier

LESSON VIIT 
38. Written Exercise. Bring in writing the simple

39. Class Practice for Fluency in Reading. — La plupart 
des hommes, pour arriver 4 leurs fins, sont plus capables 
dun grand effort que d’une longue persévérance. Leur 
paresse ou leur inconstance leur fait perdre le fruit des 
meilleurs commencements. Ils se laissent souvent de- 
vancer pard’autres qui sont partis aprés eux, et qui 
marchent lentement mais constamment. 
In order to arrive at their ends (gain their purposes), the majority of 
men are more capable of a great effort than of long perseverance. Their 
laziness or their inconstancy makes them lose the fruits of the best begin- 
nings. They often allow themselves to be outstripped by others who 
started after them, and who walk slowly but steadily.

that, these, those, are not emphatic, they are translated by 
ce (cet), cette, ces, as you learned in Year J, pp. 54, 74. 
To make them emphatic, add ci, here, and 1a, there, to 
the noun by means of a hyphen; as, ce jour-ci, THIS day; 
ce jour-la, THAT day : —

To avoid the repetition of the nouns in such sentences

Demonstrative adjectives are used with nouns. 
Demonstrative pronouns are used for nouns (instead 
of nouns).

52. Grammar.——The feminine form of the past parti- 
ciple is made by adding an unaccented e to the masculine 
form. No difference in pronunciation results, unless the 
masculine form ends in a consonant (ouvert, ouverte). 
To ascertain what word the participle belongs to, ask 
the question: What (or whom) has any one raised, low- 
ered, held out, etc.? The answer is la main in all the 
preceding sentences; but on examining the participles, 
it will be found that they are in the masculine form in 
1 and 5, but in the feminine form in the others. ‘The 
reason is that in 2, 3, 4, 6, 7; 2, which represents 1a main, 
precedes the participle. 
Rule. — The perfect participle, used with parts of avoir, 
takes endings for the feminine (and for the plural) only 
when the word to which it belongs (the direet object) 
precedes it.

56. Grammar. —1. Observe that the indefinite article’ 
is omitted in stating a person’s trade or profession: j'ai été 
chanteur, écuyer; je suis devenu professeur 5 j’étais sergent; je 
suis valet. 
The same is true in regard to titles, nationality, religion, 
party : Il fut nommé ministre, he was appointed a minister ; 
elle est Américaine, she is an American ; il est devenu chrétien, 
he became a Christian; il est républicain, he is a republican. 
After ce (c’), however, the article is used : Cest une Améri- 
caine, she is an American. 
2. The relative pronoun, que (qu’) is the object of the 
following verb. Hence que justifiait cannot be translated 
which justified. In’ such sentences the subject is found 
after the verb. ‘Translate, — 
que justifiait mon aptitude naturelle (a, ete.) 
which my natural aptitude (for, etc.) justified 
This is a very common arrangement of words. 
Which justified would be qui justifiait. 
Nominative qui, which or who. Objective que (qu’), 
which or whom. Qui never becomes qu’.

LESSON XI 
57. The Imperfect and the Past Definite.— The sen- 
tence — . 
He came out as I went in 
may mean: 1) He was coming out as I went in; 
or, 2) He came out as Iwas going in; 
or, 3) He was coming out as I was going in. 
If it has the first meaning, the important action is I 
went in. He was coming out marks the time or the cir- 
cumstance under which it occurred. 
If it has the second meaning, the case is reversed, he 
came out now being the principal event. 
If it has the third meaning, neither action is the prin- 
cipal event, but we are ready to ask: Well, what hap- 
pened then? 
Yet these different meanings may be expressed in Eng- 
lish by the past tense (came, went), while in French the 
distinction is always made. 
He was coming out as I went in 11 sortait comme j’entrai 
He came out as Iwas going in ‘Il sortit comme j’entrais 
He was coming out as I was go- 11 sortait comme j’entrais 
ing in . 
He came out as I went in Il sortit comme j’entrai! 
The imperfect expresses the action which was in 
progress (was coming, was going), and the past definite 
expresses the principal event (what happened when some- 
thing was in progress). 
  
1In this form of the sentence, the two actions are of equal impor- 
tance,
-P-A-G-E-37
30 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
It follows that every English verb used in the past 
tense must be examined as to which of these meanings 
it has, before you can translate it correctly. 
58. The past definite is never used unless the time of the action 
is completely passed (yesterday, last week, etc.). When the time 
extends into the present day, use the perfect tense. 
59. In conversation, the past definite (but not the imperfect) is 
replaced by the perfect tense : — 
IL sortait comme je suis entré 
Tl est sorti comme j’entrais 
In the narrative style (when you tell a connected story) use the 
past definite. 
60. Another use of the imperfect tense is to express 
what was habitual or usual. 
When we lived (used to live) Quand nous demeurions 
in the same house, he went dans la méme maison, il 
out (used to go out) every sortait tous les jours 
day as I went in (used to comme j’entrais 
goin) 
Lt was formerly believed (they On croyait autrefois que le 
used to believe) that the soleil tournait autour de 
sun turned around the la terre : 
earth 
61. The imperfect tense describes. 
Washington was tall and Washington était grand et 
well-built ; he had a calm bien fait; il avait la figure 
and thoughtful but agree- calme et pensive, mais 
able face agréable

Se repentir is one of the numerous reflexive verbs which 
are translated by English verbs that are not reflexive. 
In other words, the French say, I repent myself, you repent 
yourself, he repents himself, etc.

times it does not. It does in Give that pin to me, because it can be 
omitted by rearranging the sentence: Give me that pin. When a yerb can 
be used in this way, it takes the conjunctive pronouns me, te, Lui, etc. 
Generally, when to cannot be omitted in this way, as in He will come 
to me, A must be used. Then the disjunctive pronouns (moi, toi, ete.) are 
required. 
Finally, some French verbs require 4 while their English equivalents 
do not require to; as, répondre a, to answer; penser A, to think of. In 
such cases, English cannot guide you, and it becomes simply a matter of 
memory that you must say je lui réponds and je pense A lui.

100. Grammar. — Nous nous entendrons is an example of a 
reflexive verb used in a reciprocal sense. ‘The translation 
is, we shall understand each other, or one another. It 
might, of course, mean we shall understand ourselves. 
The context enables us to decide which meaning is in- 
tended. In case of ambiguity, nous nous entendrons Pua 
Vautre (or les uns les autres) is the way to say we shall 
understand each other ; and nous nous entendrons nous-mémes, 
we shall understand ourselves.

properly without knowing which function it performs.

she came in before him, prep. elle entra avant lui 
_ she stood before him, prep. _ elle se tenait devant lui

reflect before acting, prep. réfléchissez avant Vagir 
Avant de is used before an infinitive. 
will you write before I  écrirez-vous avant que je

Pantre . 
4. Je me suis frotté les mains TI have rubbed my hands 
‘5. Je me les suis chauffées I have warmed them 
6. Je m’en suis couvert la J have covered my face with 
figure them 
7. Je les ai mises dans les I have put them in my

2. Say each of these sentences in all the persons, 
remembering that reflexive verbs take étre, instead of avoir, 
as an auxiliary to form their compound tenses, which 
means that you must aceustom yourself to the combina- 
tions je me suis... tu tes... il sest... elle s’est... 
Nous nous sommes... . vous vous étes... ils se sont... elles 
se sont. 
128. Grammar. — The. spelling of the participle of re- 
flexive verbs follows the same rule as when avoir is used 
(Art. 52). It agrees with the direct object, taking femi- 
nine or plural endings when the direct object is feminine 
or plural ; but only when the direct object precedes it. 
To find the direct object,-ask what (or whom) has any- 
one brought together, joined, separated, rubbed, etc. 
In No. 1, the answer is les mains. It does not precede
-P-A-G-E-63
56 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
approché. Therefore approché does not agree (does not 
take endings, is “invariable ’’). 
In Nos. 2, 5, and 7, the answer is les. This les precedes 
the participles séparées, chauffées, and mises. Since it means 
les mains, and is therefore feminine plural, the participles 
must also be made feminine plural. The simple form 
(masculine singular) is séparé, chauffé, mis. 
It so happens that in the preceding examples the pro- 
noun me and its substitutes in the other persons (t’, s’, nous, 
vous) are indirect objects (I warmed vor ME the hands). 
In other sentences these pronouns may be direct objects, 
and then they influence the spelling of the participle. 
Examples: se laver, to wash oneself ; se repentir, to repent; 
se lever, to rise (raise oneself); se coucher, to go to bed 
(couch oneself) ; s’arréter, to stop (arrest oneself ). 
A boy must write je me suis lavé, je me suis repenti, je me 
suis levé, je me suis couché, je me suis arrété; but a girl must 
spell these participles lavée, repentie, levée, couchée, arrétée. 
Why? 
In writing to a boy: vous vous étes lavé, repenti, levé, 
couché, arrété; to several boys: vous vous étes lavés, repentis, 
levés, couchés, arrétés. Why? How would you spell these 
participles in writing to a girl? to girls? 
For both boys and girls use the masculine plural. 
When wili you find nous nous sommes lavés, and when 
nous nous sommes lavées ? 
Explain the spelling of the participle in — 
’ elle s’est lavée she washed herself 
elle s’est lavé les mains she washed her hands

144. Grammar. —In line 10 of the translation lesson is 
found the sentence il ne put, he could not, without pas. 
Pas may be omitted after the simple tenses of pouvoir, to 
be able, cesser, to cease, oser, to dare, and savoir, to know. 
When the latter is used in the sense of can or could, pas 
is regularly omitted. 
il ne peut (pas) venir he cannot come 
ils ne cessent (pas) de le dire they do not cease saying it 
nous n’osons (pas) entrer’ we do not dare to go in 
je ne saurais le faire I cannot do it

here they are. 5 remettre, to hand. 6 avoir soin de, to take care of. 
7 faillir, idiomatic verb ; tr. almost escaped. 8 or, m., gold. ® peser, 
to weigh.

158. The Perfect Tense.—1. The perfect tense is not 
used after when, as soon as, after, when there is a future 
meaning. Use the future perfect (also called second 
future, compound future, or future anterior). 
He will see you when he has 11 vous verra, quand il aura 
finished Ais job fini sa besogne . 
I shall tell him as soon as Je le lui dira, aussitét que 
you have gone out vous serez sorti 
Pll come back after you have Je reviendrai aprés que vous 
dined aurez diné 
2. The perfect tense is not used when the action or 
state continues into the present time. Thus: “I have 
been waiting ever since breakfast” implies “I am still 
waiting.” Use the present tense. 
I have been reading since Je lis depuis huit heures 
eight 
I have been here since yes- Je suis ici depuis hier 
terday 
We have been waiting for Il y a une heure que nous 
an hour attendons 
Translate orally into French: 1. How long (combien de temps) have 
you been waiting? 2. When you have finished your lessons, come 
to our house. 38. I have been studying (étudier) all the afternoon 
(aprés-midi,f.). 4. As soon as I have made up my mind (se décider), 
Ishall write to you. 5. They have been in this country for ten years 
(ans). 6. After you have had your share (part. £.), they shall have 
theirs. 7. Have you studied French long (y a-til longtemps que)? 
8. How long have they lived in that house?

160. Grammar.—JIn the perfect and other compound 
tenses, the objective pronouns (le, la, les, lui, leur, me, te
-P-A-G-E-78
LESSON XXVI 71 
vous, nous, se, y, en) and the negative particles (ne. . . 
pas) are grouped about the auxiliary exactly as they are 
grouped about the principal verb in the simple tenses. 
Examples : 
je ne la lui donne pas 
je ne la lui ai pas donnée 
ilnes’en  repentira point 
ilnes’en est point repenti 
The negative words plus, jamais, rien, guére (and many 
short adverbs) stand in the same place as pas and point 
before the participle; but personne and que (ne... que, 
only) are placed after it. Examples: je n’entends personne, 
je n’ai entendu personne; je n’entends que lui, je n’ai entendu que 
lui. 
161. Oral Translation into French and Dictation. —1. What 
did he bring (apporter) you? 2. He did not bring us anything. 
3. Has she never sung (chanter) for you? 4. If you had come a little 
later (un peu plus tard), you would not have found anybody here. 
5. How many neckties (cravate, f.) did you buy? 6. I bought only 
two. 7. Alter that, be said (a-t-il dit), he had written to you no 
more. 8. Did you seehim? 9. Yes,he has hardly changed (changer) 
at all (hardly... atall=ne... guére). 10, Did you send (envoyer) 
him that package (paquet, m.)? 11. He has never received it. 
12, When he has received it, he will write to you. 
Remember that conversational sentences with did (questions and 
negatives) should be changed into the perfect tense.

172. Grammar. — The last sentence of Art. 165 contains 
the verb to remember, which is not simply souvenir, but se 
souvenir de. Hence, — 
“Tremember him Je me souviens de lui 
Do you remember me? ‘Vous souvenez-vous de moi? 
He does not remember it Ji ne s’en souvient pas 
That is a thing he will not C'est une chose dont il ne 
remember se souviendra pas 
What did he remember ? De quoi s’est-il souvenu? 
There are many verbs that require de after them. In 
such cases, if the object is i, always use en, which 
includes the idea of de. En can algo be used for of him, 
of her, of them.

183. Sentence Practice. —1) Learn the following sen- 
tences : — 
1. Hier je suis entré dans Yesterday I went into school 
Vécole 
2. J’ai 6té mon chapeau I took off my hat 
3. J’ai monté Vescalier T went up-stairs 
4. Je suis entré dans la I went into the elass-room 
classe 
5. J’ai salué le maitre I saluted the teacher 
6. Je suis alléa ma place I went to my place 
7. Jai posé mes livres sur I laid my books on my desk 
mon pupitre 
8. Je me suis assis TI sat down 
Observe that to go in, to enter, is not simply entrer, but 
entrer dans; also that some of the participles take étre as 
an auxiliary. Only their masculine form is given. Girls 
maust use entrée, allée, assise. 
2) Ask the question, When did you . . . ? with each of 
these sentences. The first will be, for example, Quand 
étes-vous entré dans l’école ? 
8) Ask the question, Why did not your sister... ?

-P-A-G-E-89
82 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
with each sentence. The first will be, Pourquoi votre sceur 
n’est-elle pas entrée dans V’école ? . 
4) The adverb hier may stand at the beginning, as in 
the first sentence, or after the participle: je suis entré hier 
dans l’école. 
Introduce hier into all the sentences. 
The same position in a sentence is occupied by the 
other adverbs of definite time; as avant-hier, the day before 
yesterday ; ily a trois jours, three days ago; ce matin, this 
morning ; cette aprés-midi, this afternoon ; lundi dernier, last 
Monday. 
Introduce these adverbs into all the sentences. 
Tt follows that sentences containing such adverbs in 
English must be rearranged before translating. 
Translate: 1. Did you go to town (A la ville) last Monday? 
2. The children did not go to school this morning. 3. I Jaid my 
pencil on my desk an hour ago, and now (maintenant) it is not 
there (y). 
Soon, often, always, then, etc., are adverbs of indefinite 
time. These precede the participle.

LESSON XLI 
212. General Principles of the Subjunctive Mood. — Al- 
though the following tenses of the indicative mood have 
been given as equivalent, there is an important difference 
in their use in the two languages. 
il finit sa lettre he finishes his letler 
il finissait sa lettre he was finishing his letter 
il finit sa lettre he finished his letter 
il finira sa lettre he will finish his letter 
il a fini sa lettre he has finished his letter 
il avait fini sa lettre he had finished his letter 
il eut fini sa lettre he had finished his letter 
il aura fini sa lettre he will have finished his letter 
The difference is that the French statements are posi- 
tive and always express realities. The corresponding 
verb forms in English sometimes make statements, and 
sometimes they do not. They do not, for example, in— 
The postman will leave before John finishes his letter. 
The postman left before John finished his letter. 
Here the words, John finishes, do not mean that he 
finishes; and John finished, do not mean that he finished. 
Without further information, we cannot tell whether the 
letter ever was finished.
-P-A-G-E-102
LESSON XLI 95 
In French you cannot use finit or finissait in this way, 
because these words mean that he really finishes and 
really finished. 
213. Again, in English you can say: I deny that he 
will finish his letter; but in French you cannot use il finira, 
because that means that he will really finish. That 
* would be asserting that he will finish, and at the same 
time denying it. 
214. So, too, you cannot say il a fini, in the sentence, 
I doubt that he has finished the letter. That would be 
asserting the fact and doubting it at the same time. 
And so it is with the other tenses and persons. 
215. The subjunctive mood meets this difficulty. It 
is used in all cases in which. the positive nature of the 
French indicative mood would make the use of the latter 
inappropriate and absurd. Such cases are of constant 
occurrence. 
216. Learn the following examples : — 
Ideny that he is finishing je nie qu’il finisse sa lettre 
his letter 
Ldeny that he was finishing je nie qwil finit sa lettre 
his letter 
I deny that he finished his je nie qu'il finit sa lettre 
letter 
Ideny that he will finish his je nie qu'il finisse sa lettre 
letter 
L deny that he has finished je nie qu’il ait fini sa lettre 
his letter : 
I deny that he had finished je nie qu’il edit fini sa lettre 
his letter
-P-A-G-E-103
96 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
LIdeny that he will have fin~ je nie qu’il ait fini sa lettre 
ished his letter 
As there are only two simple tenses in the subjunctive 
mood, the present subjunctive takes the place of the 
future as well as of the present indicative, while the 
imperfect subjunctive takes the place of the past definite 
and of the imperfect indicative. . 
217. The perfect (sometimes called the past) subjune- 
tive (il ait fini) may often take the place of the imperfect 
subjunctive (il finit), on the same principle that the indica- 
tive il a fini often means he finished. 
218. The conditional mood, accompanied by the condi- 
tion, often makes a positive statement. 
Jl ferait cela pour moi, sil He would do that for me, of 
en avait le temps he had time 
This states positively: he is willing to do that for mes 
he has not the time. 
Il aurait fait cela pour moi, He would have done that for 
s'il en avait eu le temps me, if he had had the time 
This states positively : he was willing to do that for me; 
he did not have time. 
Now, if such a sentence is preceded by any expression 
like I don’t believe, he doubts, we deny, the conditional 
must be changed to a less positive form of speech. Use 
the imperfect and the pluperfect subjunctive, and say :— 
Je ne crois pas qu’il fit cela pour moi, s’il en avait le 
temps. Je ne crois pas qwil eit fait cela pour moi, s'il 
en avait eu le temps.
-P-A-G-E-104
LESSON XLII 97 
219. The peculiar nature. of the subjunctive mood 
makes it appropriate in such sentences as — 
Iam sorry that your mother Je suis faché que votre mére 
is ill soit malade 
because in this sentence you do not inform the person 
with whom you are speaking that his mother is ill, 
although you use the words, your mother is ill. The 
indicative est is inappropriate in French, because that 
would simply inform him. It would make an assertion 
of the fact, without bringing it into any relation to the 
words je suis faché.

222. Verbs. — The irregular verbs studied hitherto are 
Yepresentative verbs. Many others form their parts like 
uw ‘
-P-A-G-E-105
98 FRENCH COURSE—SECOND YEAR 
them. ‘These will be pointed out as they occur. We 
now come to verbs which must be studied individually, as 
their irregularities cannot conveniently be classified. 
The following observations will be of assistance to 
you :— 
1. The first pers. sing. of the conditional is always 
made from the first pers. sing. of the future by adding s. 
2. The first pers. sing. of the imperfect subjunctive is 
always made from the first pers. sing. of the past definite 
by adding se. 
3. The imperfect indicative is made from the present 
participle as usual (except in savoir). 
When the endings are regular, only the first pers. sing. 
is given. The conditional, the imperfect subjunctive, 
and the imperfect indicative are not given because you 
can form them yourself. The imperative is made as 
usual. 
Learn the verb dire, to say or to tell; disant, saying; 
dit, satd : — 
Pres. Ind. je dis, tu dis, il dit, nous disons, vous dites, 
ils disent. 
Pres. Subj. que je disc. Put. je dirai. Past Def. je dis. 
223. Class Bxercise in Verbs. — Write out the compound tenses 
of dire.

you say? 3. What would your father say, if he knew (savait) that? 
4. How do you say that in French? 5. ‘Tell me what (ce qu’) she 
said. 6. I am not going to tell you (it to you). 7. Tell them (say 
to them) to go out. (Dire requires de before an infinitive.) 8. Tell 
them not to go out. 9. Tf we had been in your place (A votre place), 
we should not have told anybody. 10. I could not hear (je ne pou- 
vais pas entendre) what he was saying.

doue est encore absolue.

237. The Subjunctive Mood. —1. The subjunctive mood 
is used only in clauses that depend upon other clauses. 
When it seems to stand by itself, as in qu’il vienne, let him 
come, it depends on some clause that is understood, as je 
désire qu’il vienne, I desire that he (may) come (I desire 
him to come).4 
2. Tt is frequently necessary to use the subjunctive after 
a negative statement. 
Itis sure that he did it | Tlest sar qu'il l’a fait 2nd.) 
It is not sure that he did it Il nest pas sir qu'il lait 
fait ( Subj.) 
The indicative il Ya fait would be inappropriate in the 
second sentence, because it would assert “he has done it,” 
just after saying “it is not sure.” 
3. A question may also give rise to the subjunctive. 
It is true that they have left 11 est vrai quils sont partis 
Cind.) 
1 all “broken up.” 2 subitement, suddenly. 8 misdemeanor, 
‘The only exception is sache; as, pas que je sache, not that I know 
(of).
-P-A-G-E-113
106 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
Is it true that they have left? Est-il vrai qu’ils sotent par- 
tis? GSudz.) 
In the second sentence it is plainly inappropriate to say 
ils sont partis, which would assert something at the very 
time that you are asking for information about it. 
Translate: 1. 1 believe your mother is down-stairs (en bas). 2. I 
don’t believe she is (there, y). 8. Do you believe she is? 4. Tle is 
right (il a raison). 5, I do not say that he is right. 6. Do you 
say that he is right? 7. Is it certain (est-il certain) that they under- 
stood you (comprendre, conj. like prendre)? 8. 1 did not know (je 
ne savais pas, or j‘ignorais) that they had said that. 9. Does he 
suppose (supposer) that I fear him? 10. Would you believe that she 
hates him?

245. Grammar.— When there are two objective pro- 
nouns, we must observe two rules: one for the impera- 
tive used affirmatively, and one for all other verb forms, 
including the imperative used negatively. 
1. The objective pronouns follow the imperative used 
affirmatively, the indirect object (dative) always standing 
last : — 
Give 
it to me, them to me donnez-Je-moi_ donnez-la-moi donnez-les-moi 
it lo you, them to you Aonnez-le-nous donnez-la-nous donnez-les-nous 
wto him, them to him ' donnezlelui donnezlalui donnez-leslui 
u lo her, them to her 
ito them, them to them dounezle-leur donnez-laleur donnez-les-leur 
Observe the hyphens.
-P-A-G-E-117
110 FRENCH COURSE — SECOND YEAR 
2. The objective pronouns precede all other verb forms, 
including the imperative mood used negatively, in the fol- 
lowing order. The indirect objects, except iui and leur, 
precede the direct objects. Examples : — 
il me le donne il me Ja donne il me les donne 
il nous Je donne il nous ta donne il nous les donne 
il te le donne il te la donne il te les donne 
il vous le donne il vous la donne il vous les donne 
il le lui donne il Ja lui donne il les lui donne 
il Je leur donne il la leur donne il les lewr donne 
These little words are never arranged differently, but 
form so many “stereotype” groups that must be made 
familiar by frequent repetition. 
3. In reflexive verbs we obtain in addition the combi- 
nations, se le, se la, se les; as, il se le prépare, il se Ja prépare, 
il se les prépare; he prepares it (them) for himself. 
4, When en (of' it, of them, some, etc.) is used, it forms 
the combinations, m’en, nous en, t’en, vous en, lui en, leur en, 
and s’en; as, il m’en donne, il nous en donne, il t’en donne, il 
vous en donne, il lui en donne, il leur en donne, il s’en procure. 
5. When y (to it, to them, there, etc.) is used, it com- 
bines with other pronouns to form the groups, m’y, ty, 
nous y, vous y, l’y, les y, s’y, and rarely leur y, yen. Exam- 
ples: il m’y conduira (he will take me there), il t’y conduira, 
il nous y conduira, il vous y conduira, il ly conduira, il les y con- 
duira, il s’y résignera, il y en portera. 
The combination lui y is not used on account, of its 
sound. Send it to him there is envoyez-le-lui la. 
6. In the imperative affirmative moi gives rise to two 
irregularities. It becomes m’ before en: donnez-m’en, give 
me some; and it is placed after y, as, envoyez-y-moi, send 
me there.
-P-A-G-E-118

258. The Subjunctive.—1, According to the principle 
explained in Lesson XLIV, it is clearly improper to use 
the indicative after expressions like nous doutons, il nie, il 
est possible, il est impossible, il se peut (i¢ may be), il semble 
(it seems), because the French indicative would assert 
positively what is only doubted, denied, or represented as 
possible, or seemingly true. Use the subjunctive. 
Translate orally: 1. We doubt that the servant (la servante) Jias 
taken the money. 2. She denied that she took (had taken) it. at 
i senfoncer, to plunge. 2 Bourbon palms.
-P-A-G-E-124
LESSON XLVIII 117 
you use the past definite for denied, use the pluperfect in the second 
part; if you use the perfect for denied, use the perfect in the second 
part.) 3. It is possible that our friends will arrive to-morrow. 4 It 
is impossible that she has written such a (une telle) letter. 5. It 
seems that he repented. 6. He denied that he had any debts (des 
dettes). 
2. The subjunctive is used after expressions of emotion 
(gladness, grief, rage, fear, surprise, shame, indignation, 
ete.). 
Tam glad (that) you are Je suis bien aisé que vous 
here soyez ici 
Lam sorry (that) your friend Je suis faché que votre ami 
is Wd soit malade 
It is a pity (that) you did not Cest dommage que vous 
think of that n'ayez pas pensé 4 cela 
I am afraid (that) you will Je crains que vous ne tom- 
fall biez 
The conjunction that is commonly omitted in English ; 
in French que must always be expressed. 
After expressions of fear, the dependent verb is accom- 
panied by ne, which is not translated. 
The use of the subjunctive after expressions of emotion 
may be understood by examining one of the sentences. 
In I am glad you are here, for example, you do not tell 
the person to whom you are speaking that he is here. 
You do not assert you are here; but that is precisely what 
the French indicative vous étes ici would do. Hence the 
use of a verb form that does not assert but only subordi- 
nates itself to some other expression. 
Translate orally: 1. I regret (regretter) that I was (perf.) not there. 
2. It is extraordinary (extraordinaire) that they did not tell us. 
83. We were afraid (imperf.) he was (imperf.) sick. 4. He was sorry
-P-A-G-E-125
118 FRENCH COURSE — SECOND YEAR 
you did not see (imperf.) his father. 5. We are very glad you did 
not tell (perf.) them (it to them). 6. It is a shame (il est honteux) 
that he sleeps so much (tant). 7. Iam surprised (surpris) they did 
not receive the letter. 8, Tam delighted (ravi) that you have sue- 
ceeded (réussi). 
Note. — The verb to hope (espérer) is used with the indicative ; j’espére 
qu’il arrivera, J hope he will arrive. Negatively or intcrrogatively, how- 
ever, it follows the general principle. Je n’espére pas qu’il arrive. Es- 
pérez-vous qu'il arrive demain? Do you hope he will urrive to-morrow ?

274. Ownership. —-The question whose? is translated 
by a qui? 
Whose book ts this, or whose 
is this book? 
Whose books are those? A qui est ce livre ? 
A qui sont ces livres? 
1. The answer is, il est (ils sont) 4 moi, a toi, a nous, 4 vous, 
4 Jean, a lui, A Thérése, A elle, aux éléves, 4 eux, a elles; i is 
(they are) mine, thine, ours, yours, John’s, his, ete. You 
can also translate : it belongs (they belong) to me, ete. 
2. Another form of answer is obtained by using the 
possessive pronouns (Year Z, Art. 350): — 
e’est le mien, ce sont les 
miens 
c'est le tien ce sont les tiens 
e’est le nétre, ce sont les 
notres 
cest le vétre, ce sont les 
vétres 
e’est celui de Jean 
ce sont ceux de Jean 
e’est le sien, ce sont les siens 
e’est celui de Thérése 
ce sont ceux de Thérése 
e’est le sien, ce sont les siens 
c’est celui des éleves

301. The Subjunctive.—1. In relative clauses, when 
speaking of nobody, nothing, or of persons and things whose 
existence is merely imagined, as when we say: “I needa 
friend who will act differently,” “I am looking for a watch 
that is accurate,” the French indicative mood is too posi- 
tive in its assertion of will act and is. The indicative 
would be perfectly proper in the sentences: “I have a 
friend who will act differently,” and “I have bought a 
watch that is accurate,” because in these we are speaking 
of a real friend and a real watch. In the other sentences 
we must use the subjunctive mood in the clause beginning 
with the relative pronoun : — 
J’ai besoin Aun ami qui agisse autrement. 
Je cherche une montre qui soit exacte. 
I dowt see anybody Iknow Je ne vois personne que je 
connaisse 
There is nothing she fears as Jl n’y arien quweile eraigne 
much as lightning comme la foudre 
2. There are many other expressions after which the 
subjunctive must be used in a relative clause because the 
statement is not about a definite person or thing. Such
-P-A-G-E-148
LESSON LV 141 
expressions are, for example, there are few men, there are 
none, whoever, whatever, wherever, etc.

305. Grammar.— 1. The English words shall und will 
are not always translated by the future tense. The ques- 
tion, “Shall I help you?” for example, means, “Do you 
wish me to help you?” and “ Will you help me?” means, 
“Are you willing to help me?” In such cases use vouloir 
and say — 
Voulez-vous que je vous aide ? 
Voulez-vous m’aider ? 
2. ADVERBS. — Observe the place of the adverbs in the 
following sentences: 1. We usually get up at six. 2. She 
never reads novels (roman, m.). 3. He always (toujours) 
pays his bills (note, f.). 4. We often (souvent) go to his 
house. 5. My friend rarely (rarement) says such things. 
6. We then (alors) lived in Fifth Street (dans la cinquitme 
rue), we now live in Sixth Street. 
They are all between the subject and the verb. In 
French they are never in this position. In simple tenses 
always place thein directly after the verb. 
Translate these sentences into French. 
In compound tenses these adverbs stand after the aux- 
iliary.

322. The Subjunctive. — When a relative clause follows 
a superlative,! as, — 
He is the best friend (that) we have, 
the verb in the relative clause is put in the subjunctive. 
The idea is that the indicative would make the statement 
too sweeping and positive. 
C’est le meilleur ami que nous ayons. 
That is the prettiest child I C'est le plus jolt enfant que 
know je connaisse 
ds that the best you can do? —_ Est-ce 1a le miewx que vous 
puissiez faire ? 
For the same reason the subjunctive is used after le seul 
(Vunique), the only, le premier, the first, and le dernier, the 
last. 
Thales is the first who taught Thalés est le premier qui 
that the soul is immortal ait enseigné que lame est 
immortelle 
Man is the only animal that V’homme est Je seul animal 
knows he must die qui sache qu'il doit mou-

345. Comparison.—1. Adjectives and adverbs form 
their comparative degree by the aid of plus, more, and 
moins, Jess. 
froid cold plus froid colder 
moins froid less cold 
froidement coldly. plus froidement — more coldly 
moins froidement less coldly 
The only exceptions are — 
bon good meilleur — better bien well mieux better 
mauvais bad pire * worse mal ill, badly pis worse 
petit small moindre smaller —peu_ little moins less 
beaucoup much plus more 
  
* steep. F picturesque. t hid.
-P-A-G-E-177
170 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
2. When translating better, worse, and Little, remember 
that meilleur, pire, and petit are adjectives and must be used 
to qualify nouns, while mieux, pis, and peu are adverbs and 
therefore chiefly quatify verbs; as, he is @ better man 
(meilleur) ; he sings better (mieux). 
3. The superlative degree of adverbs is formed by using 
le before the comparative. 
le plus froidement most coldly le mieux best 
Who received him the most 
coldly ? 
Your sister sings best Qui Pa regu le plus froide- 
ment? 
Votre swur chante le mieux 
4, The superlative of adjectives is formed by using le, 
la, les, or one of the possessive adjectives before the com- 
parative. 
C’est mon meilleur ami 
Louise est sa meilleure amie 
Ces legons sont les plus dif- 
ficiles du livre 
C’était hier le jour le plus 
froid de Vhiver He is my best friend 
Louise is her (Cor his) best 
Friend, f. 
These lessons are the hardest 
in the book 
Yesterday was the coldest 
day of the winter 
Superlative phrases with the definite article, as the coldest 
day, are generally turned into the form the day the most 
cold.

352. Grammar. — Observe the position of the adverbs 
and adverbial expressions in these sentences. The short 
ones are before the participles and the Jong ones after. 
This is the general principle ; but — 
Adverbs of definite time, like A/er, must stand after the 
participle (or at the beginning of the sentence). 
Adverbs of place, even when short, must stand after the 
participle. 
Avez-vous vilici mon chien? Have you seen my dog here? 
Adverbs in ment and other long adverbs are sometimes 
placed before the participle for special emphasis. 
Adverbs are never placed between the subject and the 
verb, as in English. See Art. 183.

893. The Subjunctive. —- Many conjunctions and phrases 
having the nature of conjunctions are always followed . 
by the subjunctive mood. The most important of these 
are — 
avant que before en cas que in case 
afin que to the end that, so that pourvu que provided that 
pour que — in order thaé nonobstant que notwithstanding that 
au cas que in case malgré que in spite of the fact 
that
-P-A-G-E-209
202 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
bien que although supposé que — suppose that 
quoique —alihough &moins que unless 
sans que without de crainte que for fear that 
non que — not that de peur que for fear that 
It is easy to see that the positive character of the indic- 
ative would make its use improper after these conjunc- 
tions. For example, the phrase before he comes does not 
assert that he comes; in case you see him does not assert 
that you see him; although everybody said so (should say 
so) does not assert that everybody said so (or that every- 
body should say so), etc. 
Translate into French: 1, Catch (attraper) him before he goes out- 
2. Explain (expliquer) it to me, so that I shall} understand (compren- 
dre, like prendre) it... 3. He gave-me his picture (portrait, im.) so 
that I should often think of him (penser 4). 4. In case you cannot 
come this evening (ce soir), I-shall call at your house (passer chez 
vous) to-morrow. 5. I love him still (toujours), notwithstanding that 
he has done me so much harm (tant de mal). 6. I shall pay you 
twice as much (deux fois autant) provided you finish the job (la 
besogne) one day sooner (plus tét). 7. Although she is rich and 
enjoys (jouir de) good health (santé, f.), she is not happy (heureux, 
m.:). 8. The enemy’s ships cannot land (aborder) without our know- 
ing it (tr. without that we know it). 9. They will not know our 
secret (m.) unless? you tell (it) them. 10. I will not tell them for 
fear? that they will betray (trahir) us.

432. Passive Voice. — The passive voice is formed as in 
English (First Year, Art. 886) by joining the parts of 
étre, to the perfect participle of transitive verbs. 
A transitive verb is one which may govern an object 
without the aid of a preposition; as, Christophe Colomb 
découvrit 1’Amérique; passive voice, L’Amérique fut découverte 
par Christophe Colomb. 
Intransitive verbs cannot have a passive voice, because 
they haye no object which could become the subject of 
the passive voice ; as, we slept well ; passive, (—?) was slept 
well by us. 
In English, the indirect object is often made the subject 
of the passive voice; as, his father told him; passive, he 
was told by his father (instead of it was told to him by his 
father). This cannot be done in French. Use the active 
.Voice: Son pére Je lui a dit. 
The tenses of the passive voice have the same names 
as the tenses of étre which are used to form them : — 
Pres. Ind. iL est battu he is beaten, he is being beaten (action 
in progress) 
il est battu he is beaten (the fight ts over ; state) 
Inperf. Ind. il était battu he was beaten (state; the fm was 
over) 
mt Def. il fut battu he was beaten (action) 
il sera battu he will be beaten
-P-A-G-E-237
230 FRENCH COURSE—SECOND YEAR 
Cond. Pres. il serait battu he would be beaten 
Pres. Subj. qu'il soit battu that he is (or will be) beaten (action 
: or state) 
Imperf. Subj. quwil fat batta that he was beaten (action or state) 
Perf. Ind. iba été battu he has been beaten, he wus beaten 
(action) 
Pluperf. Ind. il avait été battu he had been beaten 
Past Anterior i} ent été battu he had been beaten 
Fut. Perf. il aura été battu — he will have been beaten 
Cond. Past il aurait été battu he would have been beaten 
Paf. Subj. qu'il ait été battu that he has been beaten, that he was 
beaten (action) 
Pluperf. Subj. qu'il edt été battu that he had been beaten 
Observe the distinction between il était on the one hand, 
and il fut, with its conversational equivalent it a éé, on the 
other. - ‘The former describes a state, and the latter an 
action. / : 
In the passive voice the participle (battu) agrees with 
the subject; il est battu, elle est battue, ils sont battus, elles 
sont battues. 
The participle été, however, does not change; elle a é&é 
battue, ils ont été battus. 
‘The present infinitive in the passive voice is étre battu, 
the perfect. infinitive avoir été battu, the present participle 
étant battu and the imperative sois battu, soyez battu, soyons 
battus. : 
From the infinitive we obtain a number of useful forms, 
as for example : — 
il. peut étre battu he can (or may) -be beaten 
il devrait étre battu he ought to be beaten 
"ila da étre battu, he must have been beaten

438. Passive Voice. — The preposition by, which follows 
the passive voice, is generally translated by de when the 
verb expresses an emotion, and by par when it does not. 
1. This child is loved by Cet enfant est aimé de tout 
everybody le monde 
2. He was punished by his Ml a été puni par sa mére 
mother 
The passive voice is frequently replaced in French by 
the reflexive form of the verb. 
8. This word is not found Ce mot ne se trouve pas 
in the dictionary dans le dictionnaire 
4, How is that done? Comment cela se fait-il?

on (Von) with the corresponding tense of the active voice.

466. The Present Participle. — As in English, the present 
participle may be used as an adjective to qualify a noun 
ora pronoun. 
A dying man Un homme mourant 
dying men des hommes mourants 
a dying woman une femme mourante 
dying women des femmes mourantes 
When so uscd the present participle takes feminine and 
plural endings to agree with its noun or pronoun, 
When, however, the present participle expresses an 
action, it remains unchanged. 
1. She looks like a woman Fle a Vair dune femme 
dying of consumption mourant de la phtisie 
The present participle is used either with or without 
the preposition en.

471, The Past Participle. —-You have learned to use some 
past participles with avoir and others with étre to form their 
compound tenses in the active voice (Arts. 127, 183). 
The rule is as follows : — 
1. With the exception of reflexive verbs, al] transitive 
verbs and all verbs used transitively (de. with a direct 
object), take avoir. 
We have not seen it Nous ne avons pas vu 
He took out his watch - Ila sorti sa montre 
2. Most intransitive verbs take avoir. 
Thad slept (for) an hour J’avais dormi une heure 
Did they walk or run? Ont-ils marché ou couru?,

LESSON XCV 253 
3, About twenty intransitive verbs take étre. The 
following are examples of the principal ones : — 
Where has he gone? Ou est-il allé? 
Why did you not come ? Pourquoi n’étes-vous_ pas 
venu? 
Had they already arrived? Etaient-ils déja arrivés ? 
The boat left (started) at siz Le bateau est parti a six 
heures 
She had fallen from her bi- Elle était tombée de sa bi- | 
cycle eyclette .

maison 
» You had gone out Vous étiez sorti . 
My friend had not come home Mon ami n’était pas encore 
yet - rentré 
Twas born at Paris Je suis né a Paris 
Né means been born ; hence je suis né is I have been born = I was born. 
The patient died yesterday Le malade est mort hier 
What would have become of Que serais-je devenu? 
me? 
4. A number of intransitive verbs take avoir to express 
an action and étre to express a state. 
He went upstairs running Ta monté V’escalier en cou- © 
rant 
He went up an hour since Tl est monté depuis une 
: heure 
The last sentence means HE HAS BEEN UP for an hour, 
and does not call attention to the action of going up.

480. Review. — What is the masculiue form of —~ 
Belle, favorite, jalouse, douce, sotte, vive, seche, Iégdre, neuve, 
vieiJle, nouvelle, longue, fausse, ancienne, fraiche, singulitre, basse,

Notes. — The last two examples, which have the same form in the mas- 
culine, show that the rule for adding e in the feminine cannot be reversed. 
Belle, nouvelle, vieille, folle, molle have two masculine forms: beat, 
nouveau, vieux, fou, mou and (before a vowel sound) bel, nouvel, vieil, 
fol, mol.

5, You speak better than you Vous parlez mieux que vous 
did last year ne parliez année derniére 
Observe in 3,4, and 5 that both members of the compari- 
son are sentences; z.e. they each contain a verb and its 
subject; also that the first member contains a compara- 
tive degree (plus, moins, mieux). Under these circum- 
stances the verb in tle second member must be preceded 
by ae. 
If, however, the first member is negative, ne is not used 
in the second. 
8. You do not speak better Vous ne parlez pas mieux 
than you did last year que vous parliez l’année 
derniére 
If the first member is interrogative, the use or omission 
of ne depends on the sense. If the question is equivalent 
to an affirmation, use ne. Example : — 
Ne sont-ils pas plus riches qu’ils n’étaient? 
or Ne sont-ils pas plus riches qu’ils étaient? 
The second way simply asks for information.

516. Adjectives. —— You have learned that beau, meilleur, 
bon, grand, gros, jeune, joli, mauvais, pire, méchant, petit, moindre, 
sot, vaste, vilain and vieux usually precede nouns, and that 
other adjectives usually follow nouns. 
Any adjective precedes a noun whose natural quality it 
expresses ; as, la blanche neige, le brave soldat, un doux parfum, 
mn fidéle ami. The same adjectives would follow other 
nouns with which they are only accidentally associated ; 
48, une rose blanche. 
Any adjective used with emotion precedes; as, la pauvre 
femme! the poor woman ! 
Some adjectives change their meaning when taken out 
of their natural place ; compare — 
tnhomme grand a tall man un grand homme. a great man 
Un petit homie — a small man un homme petit @ mean man 
un homme bon a good man un bon homme a simple man 
un homme brave « brave man un brave homme an honest man 
These remarks are intended to guide you in observing 
the position of adjectives, which is largely a matter of 
custom, and must therefore be learned by experience.

LESSON ‘CXII 815 
Phileas Fogg avait accompli ce tour du monde en 
quatre-vingts jours! 
Phileas Fogg avait gagné son pari de vingt mille livres ! 
Et maintenant, comment un homme si exact, avait-il pu 
commettre cette erreur de jour? 
Voici la raison de cette erreur. Elle est fort simple. 
Phileas Fogg avait, “sans s’en douter,”! gagné un jour 
sur son itinéraire—et cela uniquement? parce qu’il avait fait 
le tour du monde en allant vers l’es¢, et il eat, au contraire, 
perdu ce jour en allant en sens inverse,’ soit vers Pouest. 
En effet, en marchant vers Vest, Phileas Fogg allait au- 
devant‘ du soleil, et, par conséquent, les jours diminuaient 
pour lui Wautant de fois® quatre minutes qu’il franchissait 
de degrés dans cette direction. Or, on compte trois cent 
soixante degrés sur la circonférence terrestre, et ces trois 
cent soixante degrés, multipliés par quatre minutes, donnent 
précisément vingt-quatre heures, — e’est-a-dire ce jour in- 
consciemment® gagné. En d’autres termes, pendant que 
Phileas Fogg, marchant yers l’est, voyait le soleil passer 
quatre-vingts fois au méridien, ses collégues restés & Lon- 
dres ne le voyaient passer que sotxante-dix-neuf fois. 
C’est pourquoi, ce jour-la méme, qui était le samedi et non 
le dimanche, comme le croyait Mr. Fogg, cepx-ci l’atten- 
daient dans le salon du Reform-Club. 
Et c’est ce que la fameuse montre de Passepartout — qui 
avait toujours conservé Vheure de Londres — edt constaté™ 
si, en méme temps que les minutes et les heures, elle eat 
marqné les jours ! 
Phileas Fogg avait done gagné les vingt mille livres. 
Mais comme il en avait dépensé en route environ dix neuf 
mille, le résultat pécuniaire était médiocre.? "Toutefois, 
on Va dit, Yexeentrique gentleman n’avait, en ce pari, 
cherché que la lutte, non la fortune. Et méme, les mille

En vérité, ne ferait-on pas, pour moins que cela, le Tour

SUGGESTIONS TO TEACHERS 
(The author would be pleased to receive suggestions from teachers) 
Laying out the Work. — The First Year of this course was laid out 
on the basis of five lessons per week for beginners, as is customary in our 
pest sehouls. ‘The Second Year allows only four lessons per week. In 
both there is left a margin to enable the teacher to assign some of the 
lessons a second time if, for any reason, the class has not properly learned 
them ; and also to introduce some additional matter or give individual 
attefition to backward pupils. 
‘The work is divided into three equal portions, with reviews and sug- 
gestions for examination. Under these circumstances, it will be easy for 
teachers to take the calendar of their school and ascertain exactly how 
much time they have at their disposal each term. They will find, no 
doubt, that the requirements are severe,.and that much work must be 
crowded into four hours a week. If the plan of the schoo) permits, the 
writer strongly recommends that five hours a week be devoted to this 
work, 
  Lesson I 
1. Pronunciation. —It is desirable that about ten minutes of each 
lesson be devoted to systematic instruction in pronunciation, Teachers 
should satisfy themselves that every pupil produces the French sounds 
accurately. Especial attention should be given to newcomers. Examples 
containing the difficult sounds, which are here recapitulated, should be 
kept. on the blackboard and rehearsed day after day. 
After a few review lessons of this kind, the necessary amount of prac- 
tice in pronunciation will be obtained from the paragraphs headed Class 
Practice for Fluency in Reading, Memory Selections, Translation by Ear, 
and Sentence Practice. 
2. Class Practice for Fluency in Reading. —‘The pronunciation 
of articles with this heading is to be learned in class, the teacher first 
pronouncing and the class repeating, before any pupil is called upon to 
read, The aim should be not rapidity but connected utterance. If the 
335
-P-A-G-E-343
336 FRENCH COURSE —SECOND YEAR 
pupil makes a pause in the wrong place, show him that it breaks up the 
sense of the passage, and require him to read it over as many times as 
may be necessary to obtain a satisfactory performance. Success is not 
attainable in this matter without much patience and perseverance on the 
part of the teacher. 
3. It will save time if the pupils are accustomed to pass their written 
exercises, unfolded, to the first row. Neatness and legibility are easily 
secured by requiring careless work to be done a second time. 
4. Verbs for Class Work. —It is not intended that these verbs 
should be prepared at home. When they are accompanied by explaua- 
tions, as in the present lesson, the teacher explains at the blackboard and 
then sends as many pupils to the board as there are verbs. If there are 
not enough blackboards, different verbs can be assigned to the pupils at 
their seats. See also suggestions to Lesson VI. 
Lesson III 
17. Oral Translation into French, and Dictation. — These exercises 
should be conducted as follows. ‘The pupils are prepared io write. A 
sentence is given out for oral translation. One of the class is selected to 
translate it. The teacher corrects, if necessary, and then dictates it to 
the class. 
Lesson V 
24, The Irregular Verbs. — The author has used this mode of pre- 
senting the irregular verbs very successfully for many years. It is a 
great help to the pupil’s memory. The only danger attending it is that 
pupils will work out the diagrams mechanically without regard to the 
meaning of the forms, The sentences for translation into French are 
added to counteract this tendency. 
This is alonglesson, but not a difficult one for those who have mastered 
the regular verbs. At any rate, it is better to assign the entire lesson 
than to divide it and risk losing the connection of the parts. If it is not 
mastered the first time, it should be assigned over again. 
The same remark applies to some of the subsequent long lessons, which 
the writer does not expect any class. to master for one recitation, but 
which are not divided to preserve their coherence. 
  Lesson VI 
Translation. — By carefully pruning Jules Verne’s story, Around the 
World in Eighty Days, it has.been found possible to divide it into ninety- 
two lessons.
-P-A-G-E-344
* SUGGESTIONS TO TEACHERS 337 
These lessons are of unequal length, but each contains about the same 
number of new words, say twenty to twenty-five. 
In this way the pupils are able to cover more and more ground as their 
stock of words and their experience in translating increase. Their 
progress could thus be measured by the rate at which the lessons lengthen 
if it were not for the fact that the introduction of new subjects from time 
to time temporarily obscures the rate of increase by bringing in many 
new words at once. 
When the pupils have reached the end of the story, they will have 
learned nearly two thousand French words, which, with the help of words 
previously learned, and of words similar to English, will make translation 
of any ordinary French comparatively easy. 
But, will the pupils know and remember all these words ? — Certainly 
not, unless the teacher puts forth unremitting efforts to make them study 
their vocabularies. He must not be satisfied with telling them to study, 
nor with telling them how to study. He must see that they do it. He 
must not weary of hearing them their vocabularies day by day. As such 
study is distasteful to some pupils, and as others have a poor memory for 
words, the teacher can render it more attractive, and materially assist 
the memory, by calling attention to English words derived from French, 
such as a porter from Fr. porter, avaricious from avare, epauiette from 
&paule, ete. The battle will be more than half won if the teacher can 
induce his class to prepare note books and use them as suggested in the 
Directions for Study. 
The vovabularies do not include all the new words. It is left to the 
judginent of the pupil to recognize French words that have some resem- 
blance to their English equivalents, and to infer the meaning of some 
from the context. Don’t do this work for him, or let him run to the dic- 
tionary rather than exercise his judgment. This is one reason for not 
introducing the regular use 6f the dictionary till the Third Year. 
Two Kinds of Translation. —There is no time to make a finished 
tanslaticu of the entire story. Hence the writer has planned for two 
different methods. The pupil is required to make a close, literal transla- 
tion every day, and he should be encouraged to ask questions when he 
does not gather the sense from such translation. The next day he makes 
4 written translation of so much of the preceding day’s work as he can 
do in ten minutes. This written translation must be in good, idiomatic 
English, ‘ready for the printer.’’ No special home preparation for this 
written work is expected. By doing such work every day, it soon be- 
comes easy. 
By examining this written work, teachers themselves will learn much 
as to the difficulties that beset beginners, and will naturally be led 
z
-P-A-G-E-345
838 FRENCH COURSE— SECOND YEAR 
to caution them against common errors in their English. If but one 
suggestion or caution is given each day, the result at the end of the year 
cannot fail to be gratifying. 
$2. Let the pupils who are called upon to translate stand by the desk, 
in front of the class, and use a book in which the footnotes are covered up. 
Before beginning the translation, the meanings of the words and 
phrases which are given in the footnotes should be asked. 
Lesson VII 
ProGRAMME For Hearing a Lesson oF THIS PATYERN 
1. Collect the written exercises. See suggestion to Art. 3, Lesson I. 
2, Memory Selections. — Ask the first French question and select 
several pupils from those who hold wp their hands to answer it. ‘Then 
do the same with the second and the third question. Finally, ask some 
of those who have not held up their hand : — Et vous, quelle phrase avez- 
vous choisie ? or, Quelle phrase avez-vous apprise? ‘The object of allow- 
ing them to choose is to give variety to the recitation, 
In some classes, the teacher will find it necessary to assign the selec- 
tions in accordance with the capacity of the pupils instead of allowing 
them to choose. 
This part of the recitation should not occupy more than ten minutes. 
3. Ask for the principal parts and their meanings. 
4. Send as many students to the blackboard as there are verbs in the 
Class Exercise in Verbs. (In this lesson there are only two.) 
At the same time the rest of the class write a good Engtish translation 
of the preceding lesson (Lesson VI) for ten minutes. Let their motto 
be ‘quality not quantity.” 
Caution. — Plan to send your pupils to the blackboard in irregular 
order, so that they cannot tell beforehand who will be called upon to write 
verbs, Call on the poorest most frequently. 
6, Ask for the translation of the English sentences and make the 
whole class repeat the French translation. 
6. Hear the vocabulary in the footnotes, giving the French and asking 
for the English translation. 
7. Hear the Translation lesson for the day. (Art. 37.) See sugges- 
tion to Art, 32,

ProcramMME FoR HEARING THE Lesson 
1. Collect written exercises. 
. Translation by Bar. — The books are closed. The teacher gives 
out a phrase of convenient length in French and then calls on’a pupil to 
repeat it in French and to translate it. (In this way all listen because they 
do not know who will be asked. Do not make the mistake of calling on 
a pupil first and then asking the question. And after selecting the pupil 
do not repeat the French for him.) Give the shortest phrases to the 
weakest pupils and only gradually longer ones. This should occupy about 
ten minutes.

that the French expressions should be associated directly with the actions, 
without the intervention of mental translation,

62. It will be well to consider as many of these verbs as the time

The class might diagram the verbs while the teacher is writing on the

Lessons XXXVIII to XL 
These are long lessons, too long for those who have not done their 
work thoroughly. If the time schedule of your school permits, more 
periods should be devoted to this review. 
The recitation may be of two kinds ; oral, the teacher selevting those 
passages which the class found difficult on going over them the first time ; 
or written, a selection from several numbers being placed on the black- 
board.

THE LIVING METHOD FOR TEACHING 
AND LEARNING 
How to Think in French. 
How to Think in German. 
How to Think in Spanish. 
By CHARLES F. KROEH, M.A., 
Professor of Languages in the Stevens Institute of Technology, Hoboken, N. $. 
Published by the Author, London and Hoboken. 
IS BASED ON THE FOLLOWING FACTS: 
1. You cannot speak fluently in a foreign language while think- 
ing in your own, because it takes too long to find the foreign 
equivalents of your words and phrases, and to arrange them 
idiomatically. 
2. You can learn to think directly in the foreign language by 
associating your actions with ready-made foreign sentences which 
describe them ; in other words, by saying what you do, 
3- The Livinc Mernop furnishes you with the expressions a 
German, a Frenchman, a Spaniard, etc., would use to describe 
his daily actions from the time he rises until he goes to bed. 
4. While thus learning to ive in French, or German, or Span- 
ish, etc., you are also taught how to vavy your sentences and how 
to connect them in all the ways peculiar to the language. You do 
not learn to “ parrot’ a few sentences, but acquire a command of 
all the modes of expression. 
5. All the mental processes involved in the exercises are instan- 
taneous. 
_ 6, Adequate practice is given in every grammatical difficulty.

How to Think in German, 
The fundamental idea is the same as in “ How to Think in French,” 
by the same author. ‘The learner associates with his own actions day 
after day, as he performs them, the correct German sentences that 
describe them. No English comes between him and what he wishes 
to say in German. Then he is taught to vary the sentences according 
to the genius of the language. They serve as patterns or formulas of 
speech, in which he gradually substitutes the rest of his vocabulary. 
Finaliy he connects sentences together in all possible ways. All the 
grammatical difficulties of German (the declensions, the order of words, 
the command of verb forms, indirect discourse, etc.) are taught practi- 
cally in an entirely original way, by which the learner gradually emanci- 
pates himself from his dependence on English for the expression of his 
thoughts. Especial attention is called to the chapter on reading for a 
speaking vocabulary and on learning short stories. As a practical book 
to aid in quickly acquiring the power of correct and fluent speaking of 
the German language, this work has no equal. — Scientific American. 
How to Think in Spanish. 
Among the many original features of the book is an entirely new 
treatment of the subjunctive and of the passive voice. In fact, all the 
grammatical difficulties are so presented that the learner is enabled to 
put them into intelligent practice at once. The leading feature of the 
Livinc Merson is that: all its mental processes are zxstantancous. 
The learner is not obliged to think of rules or of English words when 
he wishes to speak Spanish. ~ Mew Vork School. Sournal.
